  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f90b378d-2b50-4115-ae8b-85425ebdc03e'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1713685241" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1713685241" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1713685241?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782271499" href="/book/show/25404259-the-invisible-collection">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=short-stories-and-novellas">short-stories-and-novellas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1713685241" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7893954927781092061"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471383427i/20041247._SX540_.jpg' width='180' height='125' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>These are “Tales of Obsession and Desire”. <b>Desire</b> is something to cultivate, relish and pursue. It might be an unattainable fantasy, a viable goal, or something to chase until we know which it is. All can bring pleasure, whether guilty or not. Even desires that lead only to the dead end of frustration are usually worth the price for the indulgence along the way. <br/><br/>But when, oft in twilight, desire transmogrifies into <b>obsession</b>, delusions can engulf, and happiness slips away, like shadows on a moon</span>
  <span id="freeText7893954927781092061" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1471383427i/20041247._SX540_.jpg" width="180" height="125" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>These are “Tales of Obsession and Desire”. <b>Desire</b> is something to cultivate, relish and pursue. It might be an unattainable fantasy, a viable goal, or something to chase until we know which it is. All can bring pleasure, whether guilty or not. Even desires that lead only to the dead end of frustration are usually worth the price for the indulgence along the way. <br><br>But when, oft in twilight, desire transmogrifies into <b>obsession</b>, delusions can engulf, and happiness slips away, like shadows on a moonless night after the sun has set. The shifting shadows enhance the fairy-tale feel of many of the stories. I almost expected magic. In a way, I found it.<br><br>The obsessive desires here are varied (they’re itemised in the spoilered section at the end), but many involve the two sides of coins passed between generations: inheritance and legacy. Some characters are a little exaggerated, but they stop short of being caricatures. The pain is exquisite, sometimes ugly, but invariably balanced by beauty. There are many deaths, mostly self-inflicted, whether directly or indirectly. How and when to be honest about one's knowledge, feelings, and experiences are other recurring themes.<br><br>Modern readers may be discomfited by the portrayal of some of the women, but it’s mostly necessary for the nature of the stories and their setting. Mostly.<br><br><b>The Invisible Collection</b><br><br>“<i>A knowledgeable light came into his eyes, a brightness borrowed from what he thought he saw.</i>”<br><br>Love is blind, so what is beauty? Is it just in the eye of the proverbial beholder, created by belief as much as anything more objectively measurable?<br><br>Goethe said “Collectors are happy men.” I’m not sure that’s true, but if so, perhaps collecting is an end in itself, and it doesn’t matter what you collect. <br><br>When is it kinder and more honourable to let someone continue to believe a lie? (The Emperor’s New Clothes came to mind.)<br><br><b>Twilight</b><br><br>Know yourself. But Madam de Prie “<i>was one of those women… who are shaped entirely by other people’s attitudes.</i>” <br><br>As the mistress of a dismissed Prime Minister, she is herself dismissed from the French court. She is used to wielding great influence over men and thus events, relishing the deceit and intelligence required. In the twilight of her power, she is lost and listless at her lavish country estate, “the prison of her inner solitude”.<br><br>Other people are her mirrors, so when she is deprived of high society, she is easily spooked by her own reflection, like Dorian Gray, seeing his portrait in the attic.<br><br>Like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/389199058" rel="nofollow noopener">Gatsby</a>, she dabbles in new extravagant pleasures, and shadows of old ones, but finds them wanting. Unlike him, “She had no ‘or else’ left” as leverage. How can she make and leave her mark?<br><br>•	“The smile fell from her lips like a withered leaf.”<br>•	“The invisible wall of the silence… Even the lights seemed to feel it; they hissed quietly and wept hot drops of wax.”<br>•	“The horror in the room… cringed in the corner.”<br>•	“The evening slowly entered the room, and she did not feel it.” Evening “seeps from the walls like dark water.”<br>•	“The flames flickered, quivering as they felt their way into the dark, like someone overheated stepping into a cool bath; they retreated, came forward again, and at last a trembling, circular cloud of light arose.”<br><br><b>The Miracles of Life</b><br><br>“<i>The current was drying up, the skill of his fingers in intercepting the words spoken by the eye seemed helpless in the face of his bright dream.</i>”<br><br>This is almost a novella. It explores artist’s block, blasphemy, redemption, and eponymous miracles, though mostly of the quotidian, rather than supernatural kind - the kind that require faith. The atmosphere is a mix of fairytale, Kafka, and religious allegory.<br><br>An artist is commissioned to paint Madonna and child in gratitude for the miracle of healing, the second of a promised pair in a side chapel of the cathedral. <br><br>In the difficult process of selecting and persuading a model, then putting paint to canvas (shades of The Emperor’s New Clothes, again), he encounters many possible minor miracles, as well as great struggles about the ethics of basing the Madonna on a mortal woman, let alone a Jewish one. He befriends his young and fearful sitter, and strives to achieve the miracle of conversion. <br><br>He considers the everyday miracles of conception and the ability of “children to call forth the devoted kindness of women, and they then give it back to the children”. It’s almost miraculous when “He felt creative power in him like hot young blood”, making him “closer to the secret of divine power and the unlimited abundance of life”. <br><br>There are dramatic sights towards the end, that some see as miracles. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[As Esther defended the picture of her and the baby she had grown to love, the rioting mob think for a moment that the Madonna herself has come to protect her portrait. When the painter finds her, she is sprawled to resemble the adjacent, older picture. “This strange miracle, making the lonely Jewish girl honour the Madonna in her death.” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>•	“The heavy cloak of mist… had given way to a dull, silvery light caught like a cobweb among the gabled roofs.”<br>•	“Future and past had been wrenched abruptly apart, and looked at him like an empty mirror reflecting only darkness and shadows.”<br>•	“The saviour of the world also, unwittingly, embodied a God of love and life to the lonely Jewish girl.”<br>•	“A string broke in his soul that had so often played the music of faith. He denied God… This was a miracle of life and not of God, a coincidence like thousands of others.”<br>•	“In the life he had lived for so many years, touched by its miracles yet never really transfigured by them. And now, without fear, he felt close to the last miracle… the dark eternal truth”.<br><br><b>A Story Told in Twilight</b><br><br>“<i>There is no lovelier sight than the slow fading of sunset colour into shadows, to be followed by darkness rising… until finally its black tide engulfs the walls.</i>”<br><br>“<i>Stories told in the evening all tread the gentle path of melancholy. Twilight falls with its veils, the sorrow that rests in the evening is a starless vault above them, darkness seeps into their blood.</i>”<br><br>This is a story framed by twilight, set in a Scottish castle, with a blurring of dreams and reality. A tale of intense, secret passion, rather than a chill story of spectres: teenage trysts, and assumptions about the identity of the mystery lover. <br><br>•	“Until midnight, white light, quivering at the edges, spills out of the bright windows.”<br>•	“Magnetically attracted, his eyes keep tentatively glancing at hers, which are cool as grey stone, returning no echo.”<br>•	“He presses deep into her body, sucking the desire from her lips, falling into the mysteriously pleasurable ardour of a wordless embrace.”<br>•	“The hot wave of desire rises in him… Flames have fallen overnight, stars seem to flicker in front of his eyes.”<br>•	“The soft breath of the drowsy trees and the silken whispering in the grass swell.”<br>•	“The uncomfortable yet intoxicating sense of being vulnerable to her observation” (when pretending to be asleep).<br><br><b>Wondrak [unfinished by Zweig]</b><br><br>“<i>We feel revulsion not for the negligent designer but for the innocent thing it has designed.</i>”<br><br>Parents may shake their fists at God if their child is born imperfect, but society is more likely to spurn the child than God.<br><br>This is a story of the power of simply getting on with life, and the transformative power of maternal love. Zweig didn't finish writing it, but it doesn’t feel incomplete; you just don’t know what happens next. <br><br><b>Downfall of the Heart</b><br><br>“<i>Destiny does not always need the powerful prelude of a sudden violent blow to shake a heart beyond recovery.</i>”<br><br>The butterfly effect as tragedy. A father makes assumptions about his nineteen-year old daughter’s apparent conduct, based on a fleeting observation. He’s probably correct, but he wallows in shock, anger, disgust, to the exclusion of all else. To the exclusion of himself from his wife and daughter. “Every new idea deepened his wound and tore it open.”<br><br>I didn’t like him (or his wife and daughter), but the depth of his despair, and the path that took him on was devastating. In the end, Zweig conjured sympathy in my heart. <br><br>•	“The hills in undulating shades of soft green behind silver light… the sterner outlines of the mountains, severe, yet looking down on the beauty of the lake without arrogance.”<br>•	“Sometimes laughter sprayed up, sparkling… but he was lying motionless below it all in endless darkness, drowned in shame and pain.”<br>•	“He took no part in their lives any more.”<br>•	“Other people... no longer existed for him.”<br>•	“He was so indifferent to everything that he didn't even look out of the window.”<br>•	“Nothing hurt in his dead heart now.”<br><br><b>Leporella</b><br><br>“<i>Everything about her was hard, wooden, heavy… new ideas penetrated her innermost mind only with difficulty, as if dripping through a close-mesh sieve… Devoid of all tangible marks of femininity… And no one had ever seen her laugh.</i>”<br><br>Crescentia is another ugly woman, repeatedly described as being like a horse, and later as a dog. She’s an anti-social but very hard-working servant, who cares only about money, because she does not want to live off the parish in old age, as she did as a child. She goes to work for a feckless Baron and his wealthy estranged wife. The atmosphere is poisonous, so few servants stay long, but Crescenz is oblivious.<br><br>“Chance works with diamond drills” - a variant of the Butterfly Effect and “be careful what you wish for” (Thomas a Becket also came to mind). A casual comment of the Baron’s seems highly significant to Crescenz. “Ripples form, lethargically and gradually at first, but moving sluggishly on until they slowly reach the edge of consciousness.” She is transformed by her growing obsession and delusion, becoming less monstrous outwardly, as she becomes more so inwardly (Jeckyll and Hyde).<br><br>•	“This smooth, formal amiability embittered the disappointed woman more than any opposition. And she was completely powerless to do anything about his well-bred, never abusive and positively overpowering civility.”<br>•	“All she did and wished for seemed to pass from her own body into his; she saw everything with his eyes… and with almost depraved enthusiasm shared his enjoyment of all his pleasures and conquests.”<br><br><b>Did He Do It?</b><br><br>“<i>After a while it became difficult to put up with his effusive, noisy way of being permanently happy.</i>” And difficult “<i>to ward off his insistent kindness</i>”.<br><br>We all know an infuriating, puppy-like, over-excitable person. This starts an English comedy of manners and turns into minor horror-cum-whodunnit. I found the change both ridiculous and predictable, which I suppose is an achievement of sorts on Zweig’s part. <br><br>•	“The very fact that it [canal] is entirely deserted and serves no useful purpose makes it an enchantingly romantic place.”<br><br><b>Amok</b><br><br>“<i>The magical constellation of the Southern Cross, hammered into the invisible void with shining diamond nails and seeming to hover… I felt as if I were bathed by warm water falling from above, except that it was light washing over [me]... seeming to permeate me entirely.</i>”<br><br>On the deserted deck of a passenger ship at night, a man in the shadows tells a story of obsession and desire, of the madness that can capture a man, making him run amok.<br><br>There is a worthy discussion of what one’s duty is to help strangers, and the obligation to keep a secret, but the story beneath is shocking, misogynistic, and if you’ve watched someone die slowly and painfully, you may want to skip it. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[After seven years in a remote village of the Dutch East Indies, a doctor is visited by a white woman who wants an abortion before her husband returns from travels. The doctor is captivated by her cold, proud, imperious manner (he has form with such women) and spurns her offer of vast monetary reward, wanting to sleep with her instead. “I was possessed by the idea of humiliating her… a wish to break her pride, dominate her as a man.” She refuses. He runs amok (etymological background given), hunts her down, and eventually finds her dying - agonisingly and bloodily - at a back street abortionist. Her final words are to make him promise that no one should know the cause of her death. He keeps his fatal promise. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br><b>The Star Above the Forest</b><br><br>“<i>One of those seconds in which thousands of hours and days of rejoicing and torment are held spellbound, just as all the wild force of a forest of tall, dark, rustling oak trees, with their rocking branches and swaying crowns is contained in a single tiny acorn.</i>”<br><br>A beautiful, very short story of a waiter who experiences a coup de foudre for a visiting baroness. He abandons himself to discreet devotion. The perfect servant is invisible, and she is unattainable. But hotel guests are transient. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[He cannot afford the train fare to follow and offer his services, so he resolves to end his life. He spends all his money on perfect, meaningful flowers, gives the change to a beggar, and goes to the forest to die on the tracks, under her train. His final thought is that “he was dying for her sake and she would never know.” But just before the train hits him, she feels a deep sense of dread, and when she looks up at the sky, she notices the single star above the forest that was the last thing he saw. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>•	“Choice flowers whose colourful glory spoke to him like words: tulips glowing with fiery, passionate gold - shaggy white chrysanthemums resembling light, exotic dreams - slender orchids, the graceful images of longing - and a few proud, intoxicating roses.”<br>•	“The trees stood around him, black and ominous, but high above, in their shimmering crowns, faint, quivering moonlight was caught in the branches and moaned as they embraced the slight nocturnal breeze.”<br><br><b>Obsession, Desire, and Legacy</b> <br><br>All the stories are about obsession and desire, and in most that relates to legacy: what one wants to leave behind contrasted with what one actually leaves behind. By definition, it’s usually the climax of the story. The spoiler tag is there for a reason. For each story, I've listed the main obsession/desire and the main legacy. <br><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br><b>The Invisible Collection</b><br>•	The obsession is collecting, obviously. By the main collector, but also by those madly buying anything, in the hope of beating hyperinflation. <br>•	The collection was acquired to provide for his wife and daughter after his death. The irony is that in post-war hyperinflation Germany, the legacy has been sold, piece by piece, to pay for daily life. <br><br><b>Twilight</b><br>•	Power, fame, and adoration are Madam de Prie’s obsessions. <br>•	She wants, above all else, to be remembered for her triumphs and glories. Instead, and despite her efforts to stage a dramatic death, she is relegated to “a few dry lines in some book of memoirs or other, conveying… as little of the passing emotions of her life as a pressed flower allows one to guess at the fragrant marvel of its long-forgotten spring.”<br><br><b>Miracles of Life</b><br>•	The rich merchant transfers the responsibility for his obsession and legacy to the painter. Producing the perfect portrait of Madonna and child is near impossible. Meanwhile, Esther, the young model, overcomes her repugnance and fear of the naked baby to develop an obsessive and deluded love for “her” child, in her passion to fulfill her maternal destiny.<br>•	The most fundamental legacy is that of genes, of parenthood; Zweig refers to “a woman’s most sublime dream, the dream of motherhood”. The picture is commissioned in gratitude for miraculous healing: the merchant's legacy will be a child, as well as public art. There is also legacy in the Esther’s eyes, “a childlike willfulness and a legacy of hidden sorrow in their bright restlessness.”<br><br><b>A Story Told in Twilight</b><br>•	Rob’s obsession with the wrong woman is so strong, even after he learns the truth, that he’s unable to love anyone at all. <br>•	One assumes, he leaves no legacy.<br><br><b>Wondrak</b><br>•	Having been ostracised because of her appearance, Ruzena is used to living alone, far from anyone. But when she unexpectedly has a child, her obsessive maternal love and desire to protect him know no bounds.<br>•	Ruzena’s hideous facial deformity, is a legacy of her father’s syphilis. When she is gang raped, the legacy is surprisingly positive: her child is perfect.<br><br><b>Downfall of the Heart</b><br>•	A fixation about his daughter’s apparent bedroom assignations with a fellow hotel guest drive Salomonsohn to worse health, isolation, insanity, and ultimately death. Along the way, his obsessive thoughts are uncomfortably prurient and detailed for how a father should be thinking of his daughter. His pain is compounded by his claimed devotion, “I lived only for them” (wife and daughter). <br>•	Shortly before his death, Salomonsohn secretly gives most of his wealth to the rabbi, and his small change and wedding ring to beggars. Not the legacy his ungrateful wife and daughter will be expecting.<br><br><b>Leporella</b><br>•	Crescenz has long been obsessed with money, but becomes obsessed with her boss, the Baron, and thus his wife for making the Baron's life miserable. After his wife’s death (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[murdered by Crescenz <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>), the Baron becomes obsessed with the idea that everyone is staring at him “with a painfully inquisitorial look. And even inanimate objects spoke to him accusingly.”<br>•	The money Crescenz saved for her old age is an unexpected legacy for the Baron - along with guilt at his wife’s death, and, to a lesser extent, Crescenz’s <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[suicide <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br><br><b>Did He Do It?</b><br>•	Limpley is obsessed with his new puppy, This makes the dog equally obsessed with its own importance. When Limpley suddenly transfers his devotion to his pregnant wife and then his baby daughter, the dog is convulsed with jealous rage and determination to regain its rightful place of household dominance. Its obsession apparently results in an ingenious plan for revenge.<br>•	No genetic legacy for Limpley.<br><br><b>Amok</b><br>•	The doctor runs amok because, after years of only sleeping with fawning local women, he is instantly captivated by the first white one he sees.<br>•	“Her legacy to me, my obligation to keep her secret.”<br><br><b>The Star Above the Forest</b><br>•	Francois obsession with the baroness makes him even more obsessive in his service.<br>•	He thinks he leaves no legacy, but something stirs in her heart when he dies.<br><br> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>Image source: Narcissus by John William Waterhouse:<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://blogs.commons.georgetown.edu/cctp-748-spring2013/files/2013/02/Narcissus-admiring-his-own-reflection.jpg">https://blogs.commons.georgetown.edu/...</a><br><br></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="7893954927781092061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1640441726" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14344847852760569235">_The Invisible Collection/Buchmendel_<br/><br/>**It's a 4,5**<br/>The thing is, i do believe that Zweig was a genius. You don't quite realize how great the book is, you are way too immersed in the stories, and even though you get glimpses of how grand the lines you are currently reading are, you have to finish the book to fully understand the experience. Whenever i finish one of his stories i can only think of them as little miracles. That's what his stories are, little miracles.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2749089509" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2749089509" />
    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2749089509?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782271499" href="/book/show/25404259-the-invisible-collection">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2749089509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1493404759135626737">I have an old picture of this guy I used to know: I guess I could refer to him as my ex, but our relationship was too messy to even be called a proper romantic relationship – which means he can’t be an ex, exactly. He’s just Phil. The friendship between Phil and I weathered a lot through the years: the push-pull of attraction, intermittent guilt about said attraction, bad timing, missed opportunities, the respective issues we were trying to fix by ourselves (and for ourselves) – which often mean</span>
  <span id="freeText1493404759135626737" style="display:none">I have an old picture of this guy I used to know: I guess I could refer to him as my ex, but our relationship was too messy to even be called a proper romantic relationship – which means he can’t be an ex, exactly. He’s just Phil. The friendship between Phil and I weathered a lot through the years: the push-pull of attraction, intermittent guilt about said attraction, bad timing, missed opportunities, the respective issues we were trying to fix by ourselves (and for ourselves) – which often meant long periods apart for all kinds of reasons. But we could stay friends through all of it because of the absolute trust we had that we would never judge each other and always have a shoulder to cry on if we just asked. Like I said, it was messy, but under the mess there was something solid that I cherished. And I’m not sure when the bottom fell out, but one day, I was going to send him a message about something silly when I noticed he had removed me from his socials. I never found out why. Maybe seeing me happy with someone else was too hard for him, maybe his partner had given him an ultimatum about being in touch with someone (read: a girl) he’d been so close to for so long. But whatever the reason, it hurt in a really weird way.<br /><br />Reading Zweig’s “The Invisible Collection” made me think of Phil. Those characters hurt that way too: because their feelings are strong but unresolved, and they are confused and powerless to make it right. Very often with short stories, I get frustrated with the lack of solid resolution, the lack of closure. But the way Zweig writes make the uncertainty a strength: sometimes, it’s better not to know exactly how things end.<br /><br />Zweig also had a gift for taking a reader through a very wide range of emotion in relatively few pages. The title story felt like being put through a wrench, and made me wonder if I shouldn’t get some sort of notarized document about what should be done with my precious library should I ever be incapacitated. “The Miracles of Life” exhausted me, and left me biting my lips and blinking away tears on my metro seat – which I didn’t expect, because the subject matter is not usually one that gets my feels going (faith and motherhood, are you kidding me, Zweig?!). “A Story Told in Twilight” bottles the dizzying intensity of first kisses almost too perfectly, and depending on your mood, it’ll make you smile or cringe. And the famous novella "Amok" is a strange and almost frightening story of secrets, battles of will, regrets and shame that left me deeply puzzled: what if these people had simply said what they meant, not played these bizarre, high-stake social games?<br /><br />The theme of the collection is supposed to be desire and obsession, but those words can definitely mean more than the usual significance we ascribe them. The characters in these pages want things that are much more complex than love and sex, and the nature of their obsessions can be greedy, but are really about attachment on a more spiritual level. It is not the power itself that the exiled Madame de Prie misses, so much as what she was in the eyes of others, which is a much more dangerous thing than simple authority – it’s her entire identity and sense of self. Bob’s stupid mistake completely altered his life, and made me wonder how often such a simple blunder has derailed a destiny. The Commercial Counsellor is so attached to the idea of his daughter as his child that he utterly forgets that she is not just a daughter, but also a woman. There is desire and obsession in these pages, absolutely, but there is also a lot to chew on about how we perceive each other and ourselves – and how we want to be perceived.<br /><br />I gave the book 4 stars, because a couple of weaker stories bring the collection down a very small notch, but Zweig's writing is fast becoming some of my favorite, despite the strange things it brings up.<br /><br />I stuck Phil’s picture in Zweig’s book when I was done reading it. That’s where it belongs, with that kind of story. Maybe one day I’ll figure out why my good friend decided he couldn’t be around anymore and I’ll move the picture to a different book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1493404759135626737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8080424487334552352" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="https://manuelaantao.blogspot.com/2002/06/unforced-intimacy-buchmendel-by-stefan.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br /><br />Unforced Intimacy: "Buchmendel" by Stefan Zweig<br /><br /><br /><br />(Original Review from the German and English editions, 2002-06-03)<br /><br /><br /><br />Someone might say that there is a danger of a kind of blinkered euphoria surrounding a writer like Zweig, the mobilising of an army of too easily won over devotees, Sunday supplement blurb believers who can recognise a compelling novel or novella, but misjudge the modernist credentials of writing which an experienced critic is seeking, so that someone can line that writer up alongside the true innovators of twentieth century literature, in German terms Musil, Mann, Kafka et al. <div id="review_1011683457" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1011683457" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17433080-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420185854p2/17433080.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1011683457?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/17433080-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783863477042" href="/book/show/18472705-die-unsichtbare-sammlung-novellen">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17433080-steve?shelf=german">german</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1011683457" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4718785881008907406"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406931956i/10619464.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>[Zweig's works have been translated into many languages, including English.]<br/><br/>For me, the greatest pleasure in Stefan Zweig's (1881-1942) work is his remarkable empathy and insight into human beings, demonstrated in his fictions by profoundly drawn characters who rise bodily and vividly before the reader's wondering eyes. Written in a transparent German with a pleasant and slightly old fashioned hue, the novella's plots(*) are usually secondary, are usually just mechanisms for the discovery and re</span>
  <span id="freeText4718785881008907406" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406931956i/10619464.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>[Zweig's works have been translated into many languages, including English.]<br><br>For me, the greatest pleasure in Stefan Zweig's (1881-1942) work is his remarkable empathy and insight into human beings, demonstrated in his fictions by profoundly drawn characters who rise bodily and vividly before the reader's wondering eyes. Written in a transparent German with a pleasant and slightly old fashioned hue, the novella's plots(*) are usually secondary, are usually just mechanisms for the discovery and representation of character.<br><br>These characters are, often enough, extremists, their character traits exaggerated for effect; whether Zweig needed this effect to invigorate his imagination or he purposefully used it to attract readers I don't know. (He was commercially very successful.) The young man in <i>Verwirrung der Gefühle</i>(**) who, afforded his first freedom when he went to Berlin to study at university, went from a total submersion in drink and sex to an equally total dedication to a charismatic professor and his studies; the professor who vacillated between a nearly impossible inspirational magnetism and a cold, gray lifelessness; the totally obsessed collector of art prints in <i>Die unsichtbare Sammlung;</i>(***) the totally monomaniacal fount of all antiquarian publication knowledge in <i>Buchmendel;</i>(****) the totally monomaniacal chessmaster in <i>Schachnovelle.</i>(5*) You see the emerging pattern.(6*)<br><br>Such extremists exist, of course, but, fortunately, they can be seen also as heightened versions of much more common types of person - the young man trying to find himself; the tired and disillusioned professor warming himself on the admiring radiations of his students; the collector; the specialist; the powerfully gifted in one particular aspect alone... <br><br>Although I had to whisper to myself occasionally that the idealized extremes were just artistic gestures, there were many more moments when I exclaimed while reading these stories "That is it exactly!" or "God, do I remember that!" or "Bullseye!" <br><br>Though exaggerated, how truthful the inexperienced, insecure, hero worshipping student was; how convincing and poignant the necessarily deeply closeted homosexual professor, whose life had been so profoundly deformed by his malinformed and despising environment; how precise the insight into the self-made nature of our interests, obsessions and illusions in the collector and antiquarian and chessmaster.<br><br>There is yet more to be found in Zweig's works, like the consequences for ordinary people of the German hyperinflation in the 1920's in <i>Die unsichtbare Sammlung</i> and those for alien citizens of enemy states during World War I in <i>Buchmendel,</i> but, for a change, I'll keep this review (relatively) short. Just pick up, particularly, <i>Schachnovelle</i> or <i>Verwirrung der Gefühle</i> and watch a master of empathy at work.<br><br><br>(*) With the exception of <i>Ungeduld des Herzens,</i> Zweig published no novels during his lifetime.<br><br>(**) Translated with the title <i>Confusion.</i><br><br>(***) <i>The Invisible Collection</i><br><br>(****) <i>Buchmendel</i><br><br>(5*) <i>Chess Story</i><br><br>(6*) Of course, this is a pattern with exceptions. For example, in the third story in this collection, <i>Unvermutete Bekanntschaft mit einem Handwerk,</i> the reader remains in the author's mind as he watches the crowds in the streets of Paris, slowly recognizes a pickpocket at work, then excitedly follows him around the city. This rings so true, at least until I sense his knack for heightening exert itself, that I have to believe that the story is based upon a real incident. Here one observes Zweig's empathic process at work as he increasingly identifies with the pickpocket to the point that he sets up analogues between the <i>Handwerk</i> of a pickpocket and that of a writer, and, when the inevitable occurs, is more concerned with the nabbed <i>in flagranti</i> pickpocket than with his own wallet.<br><br><b> Rating </b><br><br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://leopard.booklikes.com/post/945728/the-invisible-collection-novellas-by-stefan-zweig">http://leopard.booklikes.com/post/945...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="4718785881008907406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3003739594" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3003739594" />
    <a title="Shankar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/96597965-shankar"><img alt="Shankar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558841627p2/96597965.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3003739594?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shankar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shankar" href="/user/show/96597965-shankar">Shankar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782271499" href="/book/show/25404259-the-invisible-collection">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3003739594" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7338929970812205527">I am big Stefan Zweig fan. I happened to visit Austria and Switzerland earlier last year and happened to walk through some of the locations he is reported to have stayed. Heiligenkreutz and Schonbrunn palace... I envisioned myself in his shoes and play acted in my mind what may have been running through him in his era when he wrote his books. I am sure I was on complete trip unrelated to this objective. But the sheer beauty of the Baroque environment spurred some great prose ( and potentially so</span>
  <span id="freeText7338929970812205527" style="display:none">I am big Stefan Zweig fan. I happened to visit Austria and Switzerland earlier last year and happened to walk through some of the locations he is reported to have stayed. Heiligenkreutz and Schonbrunn palace... I envisioned myself in his shoes and play acted in my mind what may have been running through him in his era when he wrote his books. I am sure I was on complete trip unrelated to this objective. But the sheer beauty of the Baroque environment spurred some great prose ( and potentially some poetry) which I could have penned. The sheer joy of seeing the architecture and the people there energised me. I am sure Zweig was passe' to this - living in his era with the set of troubles he faced (leading to him taking his own life with his wife).<br /><br />This above prologue is unrelated to the book but sets the tone for what I want to say about this book. The title is apt - Tales of Obsession and Desire. It has now become my hypothesis ( a foolish one indeed) that Stefan Zweig writes a bit like a Gentleman Stephen King. At least 2 stories in this book resemble Stephen King's books <br />1. Did he do it - Reminiscent of Cujo<br />2. Leporella and Wondrak - my twisted mind reminds me of Carrie/Dolores Claiborne - women who were denied their love in their early lives and /or had no exposure to the external world<br /><br />Zweig writes in a gentlemanly way of the obsessions of the characters coming from the situations they faced in their lives. The torments are written really well - tortuous really... sometimes overdone but I guess this was the style then.... <br /><br />In many of the stories the ending turned out to be a little indifferent..almost like he just gave up after the overarching efforts taken to building the tension initially...<br /><br />The story of the rich society woman who makes a "marital transgression" and pushes a Doctor to initiate an abortion is very well told...the ending was far less exciting than its beginning...almost told like The Rime of an Ancient Mariner ( curiously on a drunk doctor on a ship at midnight to the narrator).<br /><br />Mitteleuropa is an addiction...and I guess Zweig has to be part of the menu...at least for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7338929970812205527" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3098223335" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3098223335" />
    <a title="David Gustafson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40445952-david-gustafson"><img alt="David Gustafson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433738617p2/40445952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3098223335?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Gustafson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Gustafson" href="/user/show/40445952-david-gustafson">David Gustafson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3098223335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17998010260176923149">The waning days of the Weimar Republic.<br/><br/>Those at the apogee of the inflation rocket are wallowing in oodles of cash, more than enough to build valuable art collections while those unfortunate souls but one degree below are struggling to put a simple meal on the table before the next impending devaluation.<br/><br/>A prestigious Berlin art dealer has seen his inventory gobbled-up by the nouveau riche and his bank balance decimated to the bone by insatiable inflation. He seeks out a provincial collector who </span>
  <span id="freeText17998010260176923149" style="display:none">The waning days of the Weimar Republic.<br /><br />Those at the apogee of the inflation rocket are wallowing in oodles of cash, more than enough to build valuable art collections while those unfortunate souls but one degree below are struggling to put a simple meal on the table before the next impending devaluation.<br /><br />A prestigious Berlin art dealer has seen his inventory gobbled-up by the nouveau riche and his bank balance decimated to the bone by insatiable inflation. He seeks out a provincial collector who has patronized the family gallery for generations. A man he has never met face-to-face.<br /><br />When he knocks on the village door, the dealer meets a blind man who has no idea that his impressive portfolios are filled with nothing but copies of the missing masterpieces and blank sheets of paper since his desperate wife and daughter have sold the originals to feed the family.<br /><br />Page-by-memorized-page that he can no longer see, the excited blind man confidently gives the dealer a minutely detailed tour of his life's passion, his accomplishment, his pride and joy that was as transitory as life itself.<br /><br />This is the genius of Stefan Zweig at play. A writer whose short stories have more impact than novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17998010260176923149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1678704753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1747314823" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1747314823" />
    <a title="Laysee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee"><img alt="Laysee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459741824p2/4863523.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1747314823?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laysee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laysee" href="/user/show/4863523-laysee">Laysee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781885586001" href="/book/show/224754.The_Invisible_Collection_Buchmendel">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1747314823" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5298374141204663683">"The Invisible Collection" contains ten stories that embody a spectrum of human experiences. It touches on passion for a pursuit deemed worthy (e.g., art and/or beauty), obsession with unattainable objects of desire, delusional behaviors, deception of others in the service of self, and losses that obliterate motivation for living. The stories share a disturbing and searching quality in rendering visible the character flaws that we are too well acquainted with. Most of the stories are very good b</span>
  <span id="freeText5298374141204663683" style="display:none">"The Invisible Collection" contains ten stories that embody a spectrum of human experiences. It touches on passion for a pursuit deemed worthy (e.g., art and/or beauty), obsession with unattainable objects of desire, delusional behaviors, deception of others in the service of self, and losses that obliterate motivation for living. The stories share a disturbing and searching quality in rendering visible the character flaws that we are too well acquainted with. Most of the stories are very good but a couple is quite weak. The prose is beautiful, with an intoxicating, even mesmerizing, quality, especially in its power to evoke a sense of the mystical or sensual or unutterable. This is my first acquaintance with Stefan Zweig and I look forward to reading more of his work. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />The Invisible Collection<br />It was the time of the German inflation in Berlin, a time when philistines felt compelled to buy art when money had become worthless. An art dealer told a deeply moving story of an elderly blind art collector. The latter inhabited the world of his art collection so much so that blindness did not rob him of his enjoyment of the pieces he had painstakingly acquired over 60 years. He could still see every piece in joyous detail even though in truth and unbeknownst to himself his collection had been sold off to put bread on the table. The art dealer told a white lie but the guilt was perhaps worth it for the ecstasy it reawakened in the old art collector when sharing his prized collection with a fellow art lover. This old art collector had a passion so deep that the loss of sight could not obscure his intimate knowledge of and pleasure in his art collection. Would you tell a white lie if only to safeguard the one thing that gives a man pride and dignity?<br /><br />Twilight<br />The heart is deceitful above all else. This is an epic story about deception set in France. Madam de Prie, a French mistress of a disgraced prime minister, was exiled to her country estate and stripped of the fawning adoration of high society on which her vanity and survival depended. Her massive effort to deceive others and herself culminated in a last bid to stage a dramatic exit. But death had the last laugh. <br /><br />The Miracles of Life<br />This is an atmospheric and melancholic story about an old painter painting a commissioned picture of the Madonna for an altar piece. At its heart was an adolescent Jewish girl - traumatized by war as an infant - who was persuaded by the painter's gentleness to act as a model for this painting. What was wondrously captured was how this emotionally stunted young girl bonded with the baby Jesus model she carried and played with while she sat for the painting. It spoke of miracles that led to the choice of models for the painting of the Madonna. The young girl who hated Christianity was a half-hearted participant in the painting, and yet she came to embody the essence of that painting. Ironically too, as events turned out, the painter lost faith because miracles do not necessarily lead to transfiguration. <br /><br />A Story Told In Twilight<br />It was summer and a grey rainy day was drawing to an end. The narrator was asked to tell a story. 15-year-old Bob spent a sensual summer in a Scottish castle where he had a sensual encounter with one of his cousins. Because it was dark he didn't know who it was but it led him to be obsessed with the mystery girl. As it turned out, the girl who kissed him wasn't the girl he thought he loved. Even when the identity of the mystery person was revealed, he clung to his obsession with the persona he created of his secret admirer instead of cherishing the cousin whose caresses attracted him to her in the first place. The mystery of the garden in the misty dark and the secret moments of rapture and confusion were very well captured. <br /><br />Wondrak<br />This is a story about deception borne of a mother's protective instincts. A woman called "Death's Head" - facially deformed from congenital syphilis - had a son after she was raped by three strangers in the forests. When time came for her son to be conscripted into the army, she hid him. It was amazing how her fear of losing her only son and kin shook her out of her reclusive way of life to court the sympathy and support of the town folks. The motivation behind her deception is understandable and her courage at defying the military police remarkable. The son appeared rather spineless and unbelievably compliant to his mother's deception plan - not too convincing a character. Zweig did not complete this story. What would happen to her son? How would she survive? <br /><br />Downfall of the Heart<br />A 65-year-old man stricken with a gall bladder infection was on vacation in Italy with his self-indulgent wife and his 19-year-old daughter. He suspected that his daughter was sleeping around but could not bring himself to confront her. He suffered silently his disappointment at his daughter's moral laxity and his wife's self-absorbed concern with having a good time. That alienation from his family, disgust at their behaviour, his sense of impotence ate at him until his heart finally gave way. His heart brokenness at his wife and daughter's shallow lives and shame of his shabbiness eventually led to heart failure. I did not take to this story. <br /><br />Leporella<br />This is a very sad story about Crescenz, a 39-year-old female servant who was wooden and joyless but hardworking and strong like a horse. She worked for a young cavalier Baron and his extremely wealthy but estranged wife. What made this story powerful was the way Zweig gradually built up the processes by which Crescenz became obsessed with serving the Baron's needs and supporting his amorous adventures and conquests in a dog-like fashion as well as the enmity she slowly felt toward his wife. The psychology of self-abnegation and complete subservience to another human being was so convincingly portrayed as did the potential for self-destruction when the "master" of one's devotion ceased to want or desire that cloying and unnerving attention. Crescenz put too much store in the small acts of attention and kindness the Baron showed her. Her delusion as to its meaning generated an appreciation and subservience that was totally out of proportion. I felt really sorry for Crescenz. <br /><br />Did He Do It?<br />I did not like this story about a vindictive dog that was so jealous of a newborn that it plunged her and her pram into a canal. The obsession of the dog for attention from its masters was diabolical. The story did not seem real to me. The dog was rendered too human like in its neurotic craving for affection and its venomous plotting for revenge. It over-stretched the imagination. <br /><br />Amok<br />This shocking and tragic story carried the strongest emotional charge for me in this collection. I felt a range of emotions: disgust, revulsion, anger, pity and sadness. A doctor refused an abortion to a woman desperate to hide her infidelity from her husband who was due to return home. The doctor was obsessed in breaking the pride of this confident, self-assured woman who would not cave in to his demand for sex as the price for helping her. The woman was driven to deception in a frantic need to preserve her honor and reputation. She died from a botched abortion in the back lanes and the doctor could not save her in time when he was summoned to her rescue. Her last cry was for the doctor to promise to keep her secret. What seemed inauthentic was how the doctor related to the woman's aggrieved young lover. The doctor professed that he "wanted to love him..because she had loved him." That struck me as somewhat implausible and forced. If there was one act of decency it was in the doctor keeping his promise, but that came at too high a cost. This was a tragedy of two flawed and obstinate people pitting their will against each other. Human weaknesses - allowed to run amok - carry a force so furiously destructive no tragedy can be averted. <br /><br />The Star Above The Forest<br />A beautifully heartbreaking story. A poor waiter was infatuated with a Baroness who had no inkling of his devotion to her. Her impending departure spelt the end of his dream of remaining in her presence which then led him to put himself under the train that was taking her to Warsaw. I read this story with my heart racing and aching for this lovelorn man and wished he would change his mind and snap out of his crazed enchantment with a woman who had no way of knowing or returning his affection. Inexplicably, the Baroness was overcome by a deep sadness and pain at the time of the suicide. Although an inexact comparison, the waiter's obsession reminded me of the folly of the young men in Max Beerbohm's "Zuleika Dobson". (In this instance unlike the latter, the waiter had no amorous encouragement from the Baroness.) What a lavish emotion love is! What a great travesty against the sanctity of life itself when a person's obsessive desire blinds him to other possibilities for the future and removes all hope.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="5298374141204663683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

  <span id="freeText2834533999541847682" style="display:none"><b>How can I rate less than five stars a story about a man obsessed with books! </b> And Zweig is a master of presenting obsessions. Of course, many goodreads members probably understand this perfectly! I will not reveal the story, instead I will tell you about two previous encounters in my lifetime. <br /><br />First one, some time ago, when I was a student, I had to knock on the apartment at the upper level, because of a plumbing problem. Somebody yelled "YESSS". I entered. There, I saw a very surreal place! Books everywhere, all the shelves reaching the ceiling. And not just shelves, columns of books everywhere. Not much furniture, just books, a chair and a bed! On the bed in his pajamas, an old man with a very long beard, that reminded me of Solzhenitsyn, smoking and reading. The whole place was filled with smoke and books!<br /><br />The second one, a more recent event, that I already put in a previous post <i>(attached below)</i>, with the sole exception that recently I was at his place and it is really amazing! But, it was total opposite of the first. Here everything was in perfect order! He also told me why he bought the nearby parcels. All his life he lived on that street and he wants to preserve it the way he knows it! <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user_status/show/82269419">https://www.goodreads.com/user_status...</a><br /><br />These two along Mendel I'll always remember! <br />In conclusion if you think <b>you </b> are obsessed with books, think again! <br /><br /><br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3019998082" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15837405888193086562">Buchmendel is a short story written in 1929 by the Austrian author Stefan Zweig.<br/>He is one of my best writers and for whom I have an exceptional appreciation.<br/>Zweig describes the key figure of this short story, which is Jakob Mendel, a Polish Jew who has spent his life reading books from all fields including biology, philosophy, geology and more. Mendel spent 30 years of his life in a cafe in Vienna 'Café Gluck'.<br/>What characterizes the main character is his strong memory, he remembers everything ab</span>
  <span id="freeText15837405888193086562" style="display:none">Buchmendel is a short story written in 1929 by the Austrian author Stefan Zweig.<br />He is one of my best writers and for whom I have an exceptional appreciation.<br />Zweig describes the key figure of this short story, which is Jakob Mendel, a Polish Jew who has spent his life reading books from all fields including biology, philosophy, geology and more. Mendel spent 30 years of his life in a cafe in Vienna 'Café Gluck'.<br />What characterizes the main character is his strong memory, he remembers everything about books, their prices, their date of publication, ...<br />Since Mendel was not interested in news, did not read newspapers, he was not aware that the war has begun. He was sent to a concentration camp while he was innocent. After two years of imprisonment, he was released. However, as Zweig says, "Mendel was no longer Mendel as the world was no longer the world."<br />In the majority of his writings, Zweig highlights the damage caused by the war against innocent people who only wanted to lead a peaceful life. From this story, we can note that wars only destroy, destabilize and dehumanize the human being. This collective violence is the cause of the suffering of humanity. Mendel like many others who have suffered from this savagery, lost his passion and love to read but also to live. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15837405888193086562" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer8587168814068791096">in the first story a German war veteran suffering from a blindness induced by militaristic mania can appreciate his prized collection of old master prints only in abstract nostalgia whilst ironically the real collection has been sold, sacrificed piecemeal by his family to the hyper-inflation of the inter-war years in order to support, amongst others, his widowed daughter whose husband died at Verdun. In the second, Jacob Mendel the Jewish bookdealer eschews ideology in favour of an almost religi</span>
  <span id="freeText8587168814068791096" style="display:none">in the first story a German war veteran suffering from a blindness induced by militaristic mania can appreciate his prized collection of old master prints only in abstract nostalgia whilst ironically the real collection has been sold, sacrificed piecemeal by his family to the hyper-inflation of the inter-war years in order to support, amongst others, his widowed daughter whose husband died at Verdun. In the second, Jacob Mendel the Jewish bookdealer eschews ideology in favour of an almost religious fervour for books not for their content but as objects in their own right. Although it is to the Austrian intelligence services of the First World War that Buchmendal falls foul one is hard-pressed not to draw a contrast with the Nazis - from whom Zweig was driven into exile, first to London and later Brazil - and their prevalence for burning books. Yet arguably both are also linked by a shared obsessive single-mindedness, '...the enigmatic fact that supreme achievement and outstanding capacity are only rendered possible by mental concentration, by a sublime monomania that verges on lunacy' (p.65). </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1722462283" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1722462283" />
    <a title="Tsung" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung"><img alt="Tsung" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298808605p2/4992851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1722462283?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tsung" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tsung" href="/user/show/4992851-tsung">Tsung</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782271499" href="/book/show/25404259-the-invisible-collection">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1722462283" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer532932999080527556">Dreams. Loneliness. Obsession. Self-deception. Stefan Zweig explores psychology with deep insight in this collection of short stories. It is a mixture of good and no-so-good stories. But because you might catch glimpses of familiar desires and feelings, it is worth a look. <br/><br/><br/><b>The Invisible Collection</b><br/><br/>Poignant story. Reviewed in “A Game of Chess and Other Stories”<br/><br/><b>Twilight</b><br/><br/>Some spoilers but this is history. This is an amazing piece of historical fiction. Jeanne Agnès Berthelot de Pléneuf, marquise de P</span>
  <span id="freeText532932999080527556" style="display:none">Dreams. Loneliness. Obsession. Self-deception. Stefan Zweig explores psychology with deep insight in this collection of short stories. It is a mixture of good and no-so-good stories. But because you might catch glimpses of familiar desires and feelings, it is worth a look. <br /><br /><br /><b>The Invisible Collection</b><br /><br />Poignant story. Reviewed in “A Game of Chess and Other Stories”<br /><br /><b>Twilight</b><br /><br />Some spoilers but this is history. This is an amazing piece of historical fiction. Jeanne Agnès Berthelot de Pléneuf, marquise de Prie (1698-1727), was a French noblewoman who gained power and notoriety as the mistress of the Duke of Bourbon while he was prime minister. But she was exiled to Courbépine as the Duke fell from grace. She was pretty, intelligent and talented. However, these were all wasted in her manipulative ways, peremptory manner, ostentatious living and vacuous way of life. Zweig brilliantly uses a mirror as a dual metaphor for her life. Her life is an empty reflection of those who fawn around her. Yet her own image in the mirror tells of her true pitiful existence. Her life has no substance of its own as she feeds off others, in an almost parasitic kind of survival. It ends in tragicomical fashion in which she stages a grand exit, but she is quickly forgotten. Live fast, die young.<br /><br /><b>The Miracles of Life</b><br /><br />This story is confusing. So what are miracles?<br /><br />A formerly licentious, profligate merchant is now penitent and out to fulfil his vows. For two separate miracles he experiences, he vows to commission two altarpieces in a cathedral, both paintings of Mary, Mother of God. The first is complete and the second task is taken up by an ageing painter who is still searching for meaning. His muse, Esther, is an orphaned, bitter, withdrawn, lonely and alienated Jewish girl.<br /><br />Miracles were encountered several times in the story. Ranging from the natural (Esther developing maternal instincts for the baby she was modelling with) to the bizarre <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(Esther’s death resembling the first altarpiece painting) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. One miracle was transient and experienced by many <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(The rampaging mob mistaking Esther as the Madonna) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. While the rest mostly manifested to individuals in ordinary events. There were no preternatural miracles.<br /><br /><i>”A miracle indeed, an obvious miracle, one to be understood not by everyone but certainly by the man affected,”</i><br /><br /><i>One of life’s miracles, he felt, had happened to him; he had been granted the grace to give and teach the love that still burnt in him in his old age, to sow it like a seed that may yet come to wonderful flower.</i><br /><br /><i>"Yes, it was a miracle, an obvious miracle. But the old man would not believe in any more miracles....He denied the God he had revered for seventy years in a single minute...It was a miracle of life and not of God, a coincidence like thousands of others that happen at random every day, coming together and then moving apart again.”</i><br /><br />Overall the tone is grim and vacillates between hope and hopelessness.<br /><br /><b>A Story Told in Twilight</b><br /><br />This is a sad but beautifully written tale. At a holiday retreat in Scotland with family and friends, a fifteen year old wallflower lad, has ephemeral, highly sensual encounters with an unknown lady at twilight. Incredibly, he does not know the identity of his partner in these crepuscular trysts. Consumed by desire (and hormones), he sets about searching for her and decides that it is Margot, a cousin. He becomes obsessed with her, fantasizes about her and confronts her.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Of course, he gets it wrong. He mistakenly rejects Elizabeth, Margot’s younger sister and his actual secret admirer. Despite discovering her true identity, he is so infatuated with the false persona that he created of Margot that he continues to reject Elizabeth, even though it was her attributes that attracted him in the first place. So he rejects love and happiness because of his own self-deception and obstinacy. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Wondrak</b><br /><br />This is a painful story to read. This story of Ruzena Sedlak reminds me of Job. Although she is not wealthy or pious, she is repeatedly afflicted by misfortune and mistreatment. What happens to her are the result of sins committed against her rather than her own wrongdoing.<br /><br />Sins of the father – she is born with a hideous facial deformity from congenital syphilis.<br />Sins of society – she is ostracized from the community because of her looks<br />Sins of man – she is raped by three strangers who never see her face<br />Sins of the nations – she is threatened by the loss of her precious son as war erupts <br /><br />Zweig did not complete the story which is a shame because I’m curious to know what happens to the poor heroine and her son.<br /><br /><b>Downfall of the Heart</b><br /><br />I did not enjoy this depressing story. An older gentleman with medical issues takes a rest holiday with his wife and daughter. A very brief event leads him to believe that his daughter is fornicating with one of the gentlemen at the resort. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Without clarifying or rationalizing, he becomes increasingly apoplectic just from this single unverified incident, much to the detriment of his health. He slowly implodes and incapacitates his own heart. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Leporella</b><br /><br />This is a chilling short story from Stefan “King” Zweig. The titular character gets this nickname from Mozart’s Don Giovanni’s servant and partner in crime, Leporello. Her real name is Crescentia and she is strange, taciturn and reclusive. She is a little bit creepy, but not as creepy as Annie Wilkes from Stephen King’s “Misery”. Along comes the clueless, profligate lothario known as the Baron, who unwittingly awakens something within Crescentia. Adding to the mix is Crescentia’s mistress, the Baroness, a wealthy heiress, who realizes her error in marrying the Baron and becomes the shrew to everyone else. So you have the ingredients for a thriller.<br /><br /><b>Did he do it?</b><br /><br />I can’t believe that Zweig wrote this horrible story. Contrived and predictable, I could easily tell what was coming. Dog emotions? Yes. Dog psychology? I don’t think so. Dog jealousy and vindictiveness? I don’t think so. Ask Cesar Milan.<br /><br /><b> Amok</b><br /><br />This is a provocative story within a story of a libidinous, dissolute European doctor who because of his dishonest and dishonourable behaviour, is forced to work in a far away Dutch colony. The isolation did not help his abstinence but merely harboured his concupiscence. It was inevitable that the dirty old man would eventually be tempted once again. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Deprived of white women for years, he finally meets one and immediately lusts after her. She is already pregnant by her lover and asks for an abortion before her husband returns from overseas. Instead of money for his service, he makes an indecent proposal which she rejects. She runs away and he runs amok (figuratively). Desperately she turns to a dubious source for an abortion and dies. Before she dies, she makes the doctor promise not to reveal the truth of her death to her husband. The doctor complies out of guilt, even threatening her own physician not to pursue the matter.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I never knew that “amok” had its origins in Malay/Indonesian culture. In its original setting, an individual with no previous inclination of violence goes berserk and attacks people. Because it is thought to be due to possession by evil spirits, the individual is not actually blamed for the act. The doctor did not attack anyone, but he is responsible for his deplorable and reprehensible actions. But as Zweig’s writing goes, he is more intent on exploring psychology than ethics and morals.<br /><br /><b>The Star Above the Forest</b><br /><br />A short, beautiful tale of loyalty and servitude, attraction and longing, insurmountable divide and a love that can never realized. So powerful are the feelings that even life itself takes second place and there is transference to the object of affection in death.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="532932999080527556" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10688523186978461764" style="display:none">This is a collection of two Zweig stories - Buchmendel and The Invisible Collection.<br />Buchmendel was brilliant, but TIC was the one that hit home with me. Having spent a good part of my life collecting stuff (mainly music in vinyl and CD form), I've been having thoughts about the vanity of it all for the past couple of years. Now TIC came and reinforced this "What's the point?" feeling. Although it is supposedly written under the point of view that it IS actually worth it being a collector, ending on a supposedly positive note, the message that was conveyed to me was that the whole collecting thing is rather pointless. This feeling was amplified even further when reading this particular edition's addendum, which told about the story of Zweig and his own collection, a story that didn't feature a happy ending.</span>
